Definition of Shar'i Travel Distance: Three Days and Nights
Defines the minimum travel distance that changes rulings and provides the hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shar'i travel and explain the hadith proof and its legal implication (dalala).

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, shar'i travel is a journey of three days and nights by moderate pace—defined by time, not distance.

Many rulings change once a person becomes a shar'i traveler: qasr, wiping over khuff, etc. But what counts as travel? This lesson answers that question from the Hidayah, presenting the Hanafi position and its prophetic proof, along with important differences from Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling category
The passage defines shar'i travel (safar) as a condition (shart) for applying travel concessions. Identify the ruling level: not wajib or haram, but a shart (condition) that triggers other rulings.
The ruling is about the definition itself, not a hukm taklifi like fard or haram.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith about wiping over khuff: 'al-muqīm yamsahu kulla yawmin wa laylatin wal-musāfiru thalāthata ayyāmin wa layālīhā'. Find the exact wording and its source.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the dalil proves the ruling)
The word 'al-musāfir' includes the definite article al- indicating generality (umum). According to Hanafi usul, 'al- lil-istighrāq' means every traveler. Since the traveler is given a three-day license, the minimum travel that qualifies must be three days. Understand this linguistic argument.
This is a key Hanafi principle: the definite article indicates general, binding coverage.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Abu Yusuf estimates travel as two days and most of the third. Al-Shafi'i in one opinion says one day and night. Identify who disagrees and on what basis.
Abu Yusuf's view is within the Hanafi school but not relied upon; al-Shafi'i's is from outside.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred position)
The author of the Hidayah declares 'And the Sunnah suffices as evidence against them.' The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is three full days and nights, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understand why this is preferred: the hadith is explicit.
Tarjih is based on the clarity of the dalil, not on reasoning or analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السفر الذى يتغير به الاحكام ان يقصد مسيرة ثلاثة ايام ولياليها بسير الابل ومشى الاقدام لقوله عليه السلام يمسح المقيم كمال يوم وليلة والمسافر ثلاثة ايام ولياليها عمت الرخصة الجنس ومن ضرورته عموم التقدير وقدر ابويوسف بيومين واكثر اليوم الثالث والشافعى بيوم وليلة فى قول وكفى بالسنة حجة عليهما
The travel by which rulings are changed is that he intends a journey of three days and their nights by camel's pace and foot pace, because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'The resident wipes for a full day and night, and the traveler for three days and their nights.' The license became general for the genus, and from its necessity is the generality of estimation. Abu Yusuf estimated it as two days and most of the third day, and al-Shafi'i said one day and night in one opinion. And the Sunnah suffices as evidence against them.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah, defining the shar'i travel that effects changes in rulings such as qasr (shortening prayer), wiping over khuff for three days, etc. The definition is that one intends a journey of three days and nights, with the pace of a camel or walking. This is not measured by miles but by time. The proof is the hadith: 'The resident wipes for a full day and night, and the traveler for three days and their nights.' The word 'al-musāfir' with the definite article indicates generality, so every traveler is entitled to three days, implying that the minimum travel that qualifies is three days. The author mentions the views of Abu Yusuf (two days and part of the third) and al-Shafi'i (one day and night in one opinion), and declares that the hadith is sufficient evidence against them, affirming the Hanafi position of three full days.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): A journey of three days and nights by camel or foot that changes ahkam.
مسيرة ثلاثة ايام (masīrat thalāthat ayyām): The distance covered by a moderate walking pace from dawn to noon over three days, not 24-hour continuous travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum travel duration that changes rulings?
A) One day and night
B) Two days and part of the third
C) Three days and nights
D) Four days and nights

Key Takeaway
Shar'i travel in the Hanafi school is a journey of three days and nights at moderate pace, proven by the hadith about wiping, and this definition overrides alternative views.

Shart al-Safar: Intention and Action Required for Legal Travel
Defining the legal travel: intention and action required

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for Hanafi legal travel and explain why intention alone is insufficient, using the analogy with prayer.

Core Idea
Legal travel requires both intention of three days and actual movement; residence needs only intention.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hadaya clarifies a foundational rule in Hanafi fiqh: what makes a person a traveler in the Shariah? Many assume that simply intending to travel is enough, but the author explains that intention must be accompanied by action. Conversely, for residence (iqamah), intention alone suffices. This distinction hinges on the nature of travel as an act requiring physical performance, analogous to prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements
The passage states two requirements for legal travel: intention (niyyah) to travel a three-day distance, and actual travel (fi'l). Both must exist together.
2
Apply to a scenario
If someone travels worldwide without intending three days, he is not a traveler. If he intends but does not actually travel, he also is not a traveler.
These illustrate the rule.
3
Examine why action is needed
Travel is a fi'l (positive act); for a positive act, mere intention is insufficient. Physical performance is required.
4
Contrast with residence
Residence (iqamah) is the cessation of travel (tark al-fi'l), so intention alone suffices to establish it.
5
Understand the analogy with prayer
Prayer requires physical pillars (qiyam, ruku, sujud) beyond intention; similarly travel requires physical movement.
This qiyas reinforces the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خیال رہے کہ سفر کا شرعاً اعتبار اس وقت ہوگا جبکہ سفر کی نیت ہو اور عملا سفر موجود ہو۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے تین دن کی مسافت کی نیت کے بغیر پوری دنیا کا چکر لگایا تو یہ شخص شریعت کی نظر میں مسافر نہیں کہلائے گا اور اگر سفر کی نیت کی لیکن عملا سفر نہیں کیا؟ بھی مسافر نہیں ہوگا۔ سفر کی وجہ سے احکام کے اندر تغیر اس وقت ہوگا جب کہ نیت سفر اور فعل سفر دونوں علی سبیل الاجتماع موجود ہوں۔ سوال اقامت کے لئے محض نیت کافی ہے لیکن سفر کے لئے محض نیت کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ فعل سفع بھی ضروری ہے۔ ایسا کیوں ہے؟ جواب سفر فعل ہے اور فعل کے اندر محض ارادہ اور قصد کافی نہیں ہوتا ۔ بلکہ عمل کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے۔ مثلا نماز ایک فعلی چیز ہے اس میں فقط نیت کافی نہیں ہوتی بلکہ نیت کے ساتھ قیام رکوع سجدہ وغیرہ ہوں گے تو نماز ہوگی ورنہ نہیں۔ اور اقامت ترک فعل کا نام ہے اور ترک فعل محض نیت سے حاصل ہو جاتا ہے۔
It should be noted that travel is legally considered only when there is intention of travel and actually travel exists. So if someone traveled around the whole world without intending a distance of three days, he will not be called a traveler in the eyes of the Shariah. And if he intended to travel but did not actually travel, he also will not be a traveler. Change in rulings due to travel occurs only when both the intention of travel and the act of travel exist together. Question: For residence, mere intention is sufficient, but for travel, mere intention is not sufficient; rather, the act of travel is also necessary. Why is that? Answer: Travel is an action, and in action, mere intention and purpose are not sufficient; rather, action is needed. For example, prayer is an act: mere intention is not sufficient in it, rather along with intention there must be standing, bowing, prostration etc., then prayer will be valid; otherwise not. And residence is the name of refraining from action, and refraining from action is achieved by mere intention.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the definition of legal travel in the Hanafi school. The author clarifies that for someone to be considered a traveler (musafir) by the Shariah, two elements must be present simultaneously: the intention (niyyah) to travel the distance that changes rulings (three days), and the actual physical movement of travel. If only one of these is present, the person is not a traveler. The passage then addresses a subtle point: why is mere intention sufficient for residence (iqamah) but not for travel? The answer is that travel is an affirmative action (fi'l) requiring physical performance, whereas residence is the cessation of travel (tark al-fi'l), which can be established by mere intention. The author draws an analogy with prayer, which also requires physical actions beyond intention. This distinction is important because if someone intends to reside at a place but has not yet stopped, they might still be considered a traveler until they actually stop. The passage underscores that legal categories depend on both internal resolve and external action.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Legal travel: covering a distance of three days' journey with moderate pace, with intention and action.
اقامت (iqamah): Residence: refraining from travel, established by mere intention to stay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what two elements are required for a person to be considered a traveler in Shariah?
A) Intention only
B) Action only
C) Both intention and action simultaneously
D) Neither intention nor action

Key Takeaway
Legal travel requires both intention of three days journey and actual movement; residence requires only intention.

Distance of Shar'i Travel: Three Days and Nights
Defining the minimum travel distance, proof from hadith, and refuting opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the minimum travel distance (three days and nights at moderate pace), cite the primary hadith evidence, and explain how the Hanafi school refutes the opposing views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Travel must be three days and nights — the hadith's generality proves the minimum.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses one of the most practical questions in fiqh: what makes a traveler a traveler? The ruling changes for prayer, fasting, and wiping. The Hanafi school, relying on two key hadiths, defines the minimum journey as three days and nights at moderate pace. In this lesson, you will learn the proof and see how the school defends its position against other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
The text states that travel changes rulings only when one intends a journey of three days and nights at moderate pace (camel or walking).
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith of wiping over khuffs: 'yamsahu al-muqim kama yawmin wa laylatan, wa al-musafir thalathata ayyamin wa layalihinn.'
Recorded in Sunan al-Tirmidhi.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
The definite article 'al-' in 'al-musafir' indicates generality (istighraq), so every traveler must be able to wipe for three days; this necessitates that the minimum travel period is three days.
If the minimum were less, some travelers would not reach the full three days of wiping.
4
Contrast with opposing views
Abu Yusuf estimates two days and most of the third; al-Shafi'i (in one saying) say one day and night. The hadith suffices as evidence against both.
The author of al-Hidayah asserts the Hanafi proof is clear.
5
Note the secondary proof
The hadith prohibiting women from traveling three days without a mahram also supports the three-day minimum.
This is an additional support (ta'yid) for the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

رفتار کا ارادہ کرے۔ کیونکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے کہ مقیم پورے ایک دن ایک رات مسح کرے اور مسافر تین دن اور تین رات ( یہ ) رخصت جنس کو عام ہے اور اس کے لوازمات میں سے عموم تقدیر ہے۔ اور ملک کے سال میں سب سے چھوٹا دن معتبر ہے۔ جیسے ہمارے یہاں شمالی ہند میں خوب جاڑے میں سب سے چھوٹا دن ہوتا ہے نیز رات و دن ۲۴ گھنٹہ کا سفر مراد نہیں بلکہ ہر روز صبح سے زوال کے وقت تک کا چلنا مراد ہے کیونکہ ۲۴ گھنٹہ چلتے رہنا نہ انسان کے بس میں ہے اور نہ ہی سواری کے جانور کی طاقت میں ۔ بہر حال ہر روز صبح سے زوال تک کسی منزل پر پہنچ کر آرام کر کے تین رات تین دن میں جو مسافت طے ہو مسافت سفر ہے۔ تین یعنی ہر مسافر تین دن اور تین رات مسح کرنے پر قادر ہوگا اور ہر مسافر تین رات دن مسح کرنے پر اسی وقت قادر ہوسکتا ہے جبکہ اقل مدت سفر تین رات دن ہو۔ اور اقل مدت سفر اس سے کم مانی جائے تو ہر مسافر کا تین دن اور تین رات مسح کرنے پر قادر ہونا ممکن نہیں رہے گا۔ حالانکہ حدیث سے ہر مسافر کے لئے تین دن اور تین رات مسح کرنے کی قدرت ثابت ہے پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ سفر کی کم از کم مدت تین دن اور تین راتیں ہیں۔  وباب صلوة المسافر رف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد دوم ہمارے مذہب کی تائید اس حدیث سے بھی ہوتی ہے لا تسافر المرأة فوق ثلاثة ايام ولياليها الا ومعها زوجها او ذو رحم محرم منها - حدیث میں لفظ فوق زائد ہے جیسے فاضربوا فوق الاعناق میں لفظ فوق زائد ہے اب حدیث کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کوئی عورت تین دن اور تین رات سفر نہ کرے مگر یہ کہ اس کے ساتھ اس کا شوہر ہو یا کوئی ذو رحم محرم ہو یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ عورت کو اس مدت سفر سے کم بغیر محرم کے سفر کرنے کی اجازت ہے پس چونکہ حدیث میں تین دن اور تین رات عورت کو بغیر محرم کے سفر کرنے سے نا کیا گیا ہے اس لئے مدت سفر تین دن اور تین رات ہوگی۔ علماء احناف میں سے امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ اقل مدت سفر دو یوم کامل اور تیسرے دن کا اکثر حصہ ہے۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک ایک قول کے مطابق ایک دن اور ایک رات کم از کم سفر کی مدت ہے۔ امام مالک اور امام احمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ چار فرسخ اقل مدت سفر ہے۔ یہ بھی ایک قول امام شافعی کا ہے، صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ ہماری پیش کردہ حدیث دونوں مخالف اقوال کے خلاف حجت ہے۔ متوسط رفتار معتبر ہے السير المذكور هو الوسط وعن ابي حنيفة التقدير بالمراحل وهو قريب من الاول ولا معتبر بالفراسخ هو الصحيح۔  صاحب قدوری کہتے ہیں کہ اونٹ یا قدموں کی رفتار میں معتدل اور اوسط درجہ کی رفتار مراد ہے نہ بہت تیز ہو اور نہ بہت سست بلکہ درمیانی چال ہو ۔ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایک روایت ہے کہ ادنی مدت سفر تین منزل ہیں یعنی اگر کسی نے تین منزل کے ارادے سے سفر شروع کیا تو وہ شرعاً مسافر کہلائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام صاحب کا یہ قول بھی قول اول سے قریب ہے۔ انسان عادۃ ایک دن میں ایک منزل کا سفر کرتا ہے بالخصوص چھوٹے دنوں میں لہذا مدت سفر تین دن بیان کرنا یا تین منزل بیان کرنا ایک ہی بات ہے ۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا ہے کہ صحیح قول کے مطابق مدت سفر کی تعیین میں فراسخ کا اعتبار نہیں کیا گیا ہے ایک فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے عامۃ المشائخ نے فرسخوں کا اعتبار کیا ہے۔ چنانچہ بعض مشائخ نے گیارہ فرسخوں کا ذکر کیا ہے بعض نے اٹھارہ کا بعض نے پندرہ ( واللہ اعلم ) اور دریا میں خشکی کی رفتار معتبر نہیں۔ لا يعتبر السير فى الماء معناه لا يعتبر به السير فى البر، فاما المعتبر في البحر فما يليق بحاله كما في الجبل۔ صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ دریا کے اندر اگر کشتی سے سفر کیا جائے تو اس کے حال کے مناسب کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا یعنی ہوا کے مطابق۔
The travel by which rulings change is that one intends a journey of three days and their nights with the pace of camels and walking of feet, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The resident wipes for a complete day and night, and the traveler for three days and their nights.' The concession encompasses the kind, and from its necessity is the generalization of the estimation. Abu Yusuf estimated it as two days and most of the third, and al-Shafi'i in one saying as one day and night. The Sunnah is sufficient as evidence against them both. Translation: The travel that changes rulings is that one intends the pace of three days and nights by camel or walking, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said the resident wipes for a full day and night, and the traveler for three days and nights. The concession is general to all travelers, and from its necessity is the generalization of estimation. Abu Yusuf set the travel distance as two full days and most of the third, and al-Shafi'i set it as one day and night. The mentioned hadith is sufficient evidence against both. Explanation: Al-Quduri said that the travel that changes rulings is that a person intends a three-day, three-night journey, with the pace of camels, or walking, or ox-cart. It should be kept in mind that the shortest day of the year in each country is considered. For example, in our region of North India, the shortest day occurs in deep winter. Also, the travel is not meant to be 24 hours continuously, but rather walking from morning to the time of zawaal (declining sun) each day, because continuously traveling 24 hours is neither in human capacity nor in the strength of the riding animal. In any case, the distance covered from morning to zawaal, arriving at a stage and resting over three days and nights, is the travel distance. The hadith of the Prophet 'yamsahu al-muqim...' is used as evidence for the estimation of three days and nights. The reasoning is that the 'al-' in 'al-musafir' is for generality (istighraq), so the concession of wiping applies to every traveler, meaning every traveler can wipe for three days and nights. This is only possible if the minimum travel period is three days and nights. If the minimum were less, then it would not be possible for every traveler to wipe for three days and nights, but the hadith establishes the ability for every traveler, so the minimum travel period is three days and nights. Also, our school is supported by the hadith: 'A woman should not travel for more than three days and nights unless she has her husband or a mahram relative with her.' The word 'above' (fawqa) is extra, just as in 'faḍribū fawqa al-a‘nāq' the word 'fawqa' is extra. The meaning of the hadith is that no woman should travel for three days and nights unless with her husband or a mahram. It is agreed that a woman is allowed to travel for less than this period without a mahram. Since the hadith prohibits a woman from traveling for three days and nights without a mahram, the travel period is three days and nights. Among Hanafi scholars, Imam Abu Yusuf said the minimum travel is two complete days and most of the third. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, in one saying, it is one day and night. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad said four farsakhs is the minimum travel; this is also a saying of al-Shafi'i. The author of al-Hidayah says that the hadith we presented is evidence against both opposing views. The moderate pace is considered: 'The mentioned pace is the middle pace.' From Abu Hanifa, estimation by stages (manazil) is narrated, which is close to the first. There is no consideration of farsakhs; that is the correct view. Al-Quduri says that by the pace of camels or feet, the moderate pace is meant, neither very fast nor very slow, but the middle pace. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the minimum travel is three stages; i.e., if someone starts travel with the intention of three stages, he is legally a traveler. The author of al-Hidayah says that this saying of Imam Abu Hanifa is also close to the first saying. A person usually travels one stage in a day, especially in short days, so expressing the travel period as three days or three stages is the same. The author of al-Hidayah says that according to the correct saying, the distance is not determined by farsakhs; one farsakh is three miles. The majority of the mashayikh considered farsakhs; some mentioned 11, some 18, some 15. And in the sea, land pace is not considered. 'Pace in water is not considered; its meaning is that the pace on land is not considered for it. As for what is considered in the sea, it is what is suitable for its situation, as in the mountain.' The issue is that if one travels by boat in the sea, the pace appropriate to its condition is considered, i.e., according to the wind.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah establishes the minimum distance for shar'i travel in the Hanafi school. The author, using the matn of al-Quduri, states that travel changes rulings only when one intends a journey of three days and nights at a moderate pace (camel or walking). The primary proof is the hadith of wiping over khuffs: the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed the traveler to wipe for three days and nights, which implies that the minimum travel period is three days because the hadith's general wording (al-musafir) includes all travelers. If the minimum were less, some travelers would not be able to wipe for the full three days. A second proof is the hadith prohibiting women from traveling three days without a mahram. The author then refutes the views of Abu Yusuf (two days and most of the third) and al-Shafi'i (one day and night), asserting that the hadith is sufficient evidence against them. He also discusses that the pace is moderate, not extreme, and that sea travel is judged by its own standard (wind and conditions). The passage includes an intra-Hanafi difference (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf) and notes that the relied-upon position is three days, with stages (manazil) as an alternative expression. The majority of later scholars also used farsakhs, but the primary criterion remains days of travel.

Key Terms
مسح (mash): Wiping over khuffs during wudu; the concession for travelers extends to three days and nights.
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance equal to approximately 3 miles; used by some later Hanafi scholars but not the primary criterion.
منزل (manzil): A stage of travel; typically one day's journey at moderate pace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum distance that constitutes shar'i travel?
A) One day's journey
B) Two days' journey
C) Three days and nights at moderate pace
D) Four farsakhs

Key Takeaway
The minimum shar'i travel distance for the Hanafi school is three days and nights at moderate pace, proven by the generality of the hadith on wiping.

Minimum Travel Distance: Three Days at Moderate Speed
Defines travel distance using hadith and madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on minimum travel distance for qasr, explain the hadith evidence with the linguistic argument on 'fawqa', and compare it with the views of other madhhabs.

Core Idea
Three days at moderate speed: the Hanafi threshold for travel concessions, proven by the hadith on women's travel.

This passage establishes the Hanafi school's definition of travel distance using a hadith that prohibits women from traveling without a mahram for more than three days. By analyzing the word 'fawqa' as redundant, the author argues that the prohibition directly sets the travel threshold at exactly three days. The passage then compares this with other madhhabs and clarifies the measurement method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State the minimum travel distance according to Imam Abu Hanifa: three days and three nights at moderate speed.
Also narrated as three stages (manazil).
2
Examine the hadith proof
Read the hadith: 'لا تسافر المرأة فوق ثلاثة أيام إلا ومعها زوجها أو ذو محرم منها' and understand the linguistic analysis that makes 'fawqa' redundant, so the prohibition applies to exactly three days.
The same analysis is used in Quranic verse 'فَاضْرِبُوا فَوْقَ الْأَعْنَاقِ' where 'fawqa' is also considered redundant.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Abu Yusuf holds the minimum is two complete days and most of the third; Imam Muhammad follows Abu Hanifa.
The mufta bihi (relied upon) is Abu Hanifa's three days.
4
Compare with other madhhabs
Al-Shafi'i has two views: one day and one night or four farsakh; Malik and Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold four farsakh. The author of al-Hidayah says the hadith is evidence against these views.
The passage also rejects farsakh as a reliable measure due to inconsistency (11, 15, or 18 farsakh reported by later scholars).

باب صلوة المسافر ہمارے مذہب کی تائید اس حدیث سے بھی ہوتی ہے: لا تسافر المرأة فوق ثلاثة أيام إلا ومعها زوجها أو ذو محرم منها۔ حدیث میں لفظ فوق زائد ہے جیسے فَاضْرِبُوا فَوْقَ الْأَعْنَاقِ میں لفظ فوق زائد ہے۔ اب حدیث کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کوئی عورت تین دن اور تین رات سفر نہ کرے مگر یہ کہ اس کے ساتھ اس کا شوہر ہو یا کوئی ذی رحم محرم ہو۔ یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ عورت کو مدت سفر سے کم بغیر محرم کے سفر کرنے کی اجازت ہے۔ پس چونکہ حدیث میں تین دن اور تین رات عورت کو بغیر محرم کے سفر کرنے سے منع کیا گیا ہے، اس لئے مدت سفر تین دن اور تین رات ہوگی۔
علماء احناف میں سے امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ اقل مدت سفر دو یوم کامل اور تیسرے دن کا اکثر حصہ ہے۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک ایک قول کے مطابق ایک دن اور ایک رات کم از کم سفر کی مدت ہے۔ امام مالک اور امام احمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ چار فرسخ اقل مدت سفر ہے، اور یہ بھی ایک قول امام شافعی کا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ ہماری پیش کردہ حدیث دونوں مخالف اقوال کے خلاف حجت ہے۔
متوسط رفتار معتبر ہے: السير المذكور هو الوسط، وعن أبي حنيفة التقدير بالمراحل وهو قريب من الأول، ولا معتبر بالفراسخ. جمہور اور جس رفتار کا ذکر کیا گیا ہے وہ اوسط درجہ کی رفتار ہے۔ اور ابوحنیفہ سے مرحلوں کے ساتھ اندازہ مروی ہے، اور یہ قول اول سے قریب ہے، اور فرسخوں کے ساتھ اندازہ کرنا معتبر نہیں ہے، یہی صحیح ہے۔ صاحب قدوری کہتے ہیں کہ اونٹ یا قدموں کی رفتار میں معتدل اور اوسط درجہ کی رفتار مراد ہے، نہ بہت تیز ہو اور نہ بہت سست بلکہ درمیانی چال ہو۔ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایک روایت ہے کہ ادنی مدت سفر تین منزل ہیں، یعنی اگر کسی نے تین منزل کے ارادے سے سفر شروع کیا تو وہ شرعاً مسافر کہلائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام صاحب کا یہ قول بھی قول اول سے قریب ہے، کیونکہ انسان عادتاً ایک دن میں ایک منزل کا سفر کرتا ہے بالخصوص چھوٹے دنوں میں، لہذا مدت سفر تین دن بیان کرنا یا تین منزل بیان کرنا ایک ہی بات ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا ہے کہ صحیح قول کے مطابق مدت سفر کی تعیین میں فراسخ کا اعتبار نہیں کیا گیا ہے، ایک فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے۔ عامۃ المشائخ نے فرسخوں کا اعتبار کیا ہے، چنانچہ بعض مشائخ نے گیارہ فرسخوں کا ذکر کیا ہے، بعض نے اٹھارہ کا، اور بعض نے پندرہ کا۔ (والعلم عند الله)
Chapter on the prayer of the traveler. Our school is also supported by this hadith: 'A woman should not travel for more than three days except with her husband or a mahram relative.' In the hadith, the word 'fawqa' is extra, just as in 'fadribu fawqa al-a'naq' the word 'fawqa' is extra. Now the meaning of the hadith is that a woman should not travel for three days and three nights unless her husband or a mahram relative is with her. It is agreed that a woman is permitted to travel less than that period without a mahram. Since the hadith prohibits a woman from traveling without a mahram for three days and three nights, the travel period is three days and three nights. Among Hanafi scholars, Imam Abu Yusuf said the minimum travel period is two complete days and most of the third. According to one view of Imam al-Shafi'i, the minimum is one day and one night. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad said four farsakh is the minimum, and that is also a view of al-Shafi'i. The author of al-Hidayah says that the hadith we presented is evidence against both opposing views. The moderate speed is considered: The mentioned travel is the moderate one. From Abu Hanifa there is estimation by stages (marahil), and it is close to the first. And estimation by farsakh is not considered. The majority say the speed mentioned is the average speed. From Abu Hanifa it is narrated that the minimum travel distance is three stages; if someone starts a journey intending three stages, he is legally a traveler. The author of al-Hidayah says that the Imam's view is close to the first, because a person usually travels one stage per day, especially in short days, so saying three days or three stages is the same. The author of al-Hidayah said that according to the correct view, farsakh are not considered in determining travel distance; one farsakh is three miles. The majority of scholars considered farsakh; some mentioned eleven farsakh, some eighteen, and some fifteen. (And Allah knows best).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the minimum travel distance that entitles a person to the concessions of travel (rukhsah), such as qasr of prayer and breaking the fast. The author begins by citing a hadith which prohibits a woman from traveling for more than three days without a mahram. By analyzing the word 'fawqa' as redundant, he argues that the prohibition is for exactly three days, thus establishing that the minimum distance for which travel concessions apply is three days' journey. He then presents a hadith-based proof. The passage then enumerates the positions of other scholars: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that it is two full days plus most of the third; Imam al-Shafi'i has two views – one day and one night, and also four farsakh; Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold four farsakh. The author of al-Hidayah asserts that the hadith is decisive against these views. Next, the passage clarifies that the speed considered in measuring three days is the moderate, average speed – not very fast or slow. Abu Hanifa also expressed this as three stages (marahil), since one stage is typically one day's travel. The author dismisses the use of farsakh as a reliable measure for this purpose, noting that the majority of later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) did adopt farsakh but with disagreement on the exact number (11, 15, or 18 farsakh). The lesson establishes that for Hanafis, the travel distance is defined by days at moderate speed, not by arbitrary linear measurements. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad) since it is the relied-upon position of the school.

Key Terms
فوق (fawqa): In this context, a redundant particle used for emphasis, not to indicate excess.
منزل (manzil): A stage; roughly one day's journey at moderate speed.
فرسخ (farsakh): A linear measure equal to three miles, used by some scholars for travel distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum travel distance for qasr and other travel concessions?
A) One day and one night
B) Two complete days and most of the third
C) Three days and three nights at moderate speed
D) Four farsakh

Key Takeaway
The minimum travel distance for qasr in the Hanafi school is three days and three nights at moderate speed, derived from the hadith on women's travel and supported by the concept of stages.

Speed in Sea Travel for Travel Concessions
Explains that sea travel's speed is judged by its own conditions, not by land speed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for measuring travel distance by sea and identify why land speed is not considered.

Core Idea
Sea travel concessions depend on actual time at sea speed, not compared to land.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies that the speed of travel for determining the minimum journey (safar) that qualifies for concessions is not taken from land speed but from what is appropriate for the sea. This is analogous to mountain travel, where terrain affects speed. The ruling ensures that travel concessions are applied fairly based on the actual mode of travel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Note that the passage states: in sea travel, land speed is not considered; rather, the speed appropriate to the sea's condition is used.
The key phrase: 'فما يليق بحاله' (what befits its condition).
2
Understand the analogy
The ruling analogizes sea travel to mountain travel (كما في الجبل) where the same distance may take longer due to terrain, and the speed is measured by what is normal for that terrain.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on the common illa of condition-dependent speed.
3
Apply the example
Consider a destination reachable in 3 days by land but only 2 days by sea under moderate conditions. The sea traveler does not qualify for travel concessions because the sea journey takes less than 3 days.
The measure is the actual time taken under normal conditions for that mode.
4
Recognize the principle
The general principle: for travel concessions, the journey must be of a certain duration (3 days) at a moderate speed under the normal conditions of that travel mode.
Unfavorable winds or obstacles are not considered; only moderate conditions.
5
Note cross-madhhab relevance
This is a distinctly Hanafi position; other schools may use different criteria (e.g., distance-based). The passage emphasizes the contextual nature of travel.
The authority weight is from the later Hanafi texts (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

In the sea, land speed is not considered: 'The travel in water is not considered, meaning that the travel on land is not considered along with it. As for what is considered in the sea, it is what befits its condition, just as in the mountains.' The majority say that speed in the sea is not considered. The meaning is that sea speed is not compared to land speed. As for what is considered in the sea, it is what is appropriate to its condition, like in the mountains. The case is that if one travels by ship in the sea, the appropriate condition is considered. If the wind is neither favorable nor unfavorable, then the distance covered in three days and three nights will be called the travel period. Just as in mountain travel, the distance of three days and three nights is considered, even if on flat land that distance would be covered in less time. The text's meaning is that in sea travel, land speed is not considered. For example, to reach a place there are two routes: by sea and by land. By land, the distance is three days and three nights; by sea, it is two days. So if a person travels by land, he gets the travel concessions; if by sea, he does not.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how to measure travel distance for concessions when traveling by sea. The author clarifies that the speed of travel (sayr) for determining whether a journey qualifies as 'travel' (safar) is not taken from land speed but from what is reasonable for sea travel. This is analogous to mountain travel, where the same distance may take longer than on flat land. The principle is that the three-day journey is measured by the actual time taken under the conditions, not by comparing to an ideal flat land speed. An example is given: if a destination is two days by sea but three days by land, a person sailing there in two days would not get travel concessions because the sea route is shorter in time. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi view that travel concessions apply to journeys of three days at moderate speed, but the speed is contextual to the terrain. The authority weight is from the commentaries on al-Hidayah, representing the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
السير في الماء (al-sayr fi al-ma'): Travel by sea, where speed is determined by nautical conditions, not by land units.
حالة (halah): The condition or circumstance, such as wind, terrain, etc., that affects travel speed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Travel concessions are determined by the actual travel time under normal conditions for that mode, not by comparing to land.

Status of Qasr: Concession of Reduction vs. Concession of Permission
Establishes Hanafi view that qasr is a mandatory reduction, not just a permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that qasr is wajib and explain the difference between rukhsat isqat and rukhsat tarkhis.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, qasr is not optional—it is a compulsory reduction of the fard itself.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the nature of qasr (shortening prayer) during travel. The Hanafi school, following Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that the traveler's fard is genuinely reduced from four to two rak'ahs; qasr is therefore an obligatory reduction (rukhsat isqat). The Shafi'i school, by contrast, views qasr as a permissible concession (rukhsat tarkhis), meaning the original obligation of four remains but the traveler may shorten. This lesson unpacks the proofs and practical implications of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Read al-Quduri's matn: 'The traveler's fard in four-rak'ah prayers is two rak'ahs; he should not add.' This is the explicit Hanafi position: qasr is wajib.
Identify the term 'rukhsat isqat' as the key concept.
2
Identify the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the fard remains four, and qasr is a concession (rukhsat tarkhis) with completion being better. Note that Ahmad and one view of Malik agree.
Distinguish between permission and reduction.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil
The second pair (shaf' thani) is neither made up (qada') nor sinful to omit. This indicates it is nafl, not fard. Contrast with fasting, which must be made up if broken.
The absence of qada' and sin are the core indicators of nafl.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proofs
Three proofs: (1) analogy to fasting; (2) Quranic 'la junah' suggesting permission; (3) hadith of charity where qasr is called a sadaqah to be accepted.
Consider how Hanafis could respond: 'la junah' can indicate obligation in context; the hadith means accept the ruling as a gift, not that it's optional.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Hanafi: traveler must pray two; praying four is makruh tahrimi (or invalid). Shafi'i: traveler may choose; four is better. This affects daily practice on journeys.
Practical consequence: a Hanafi must perform sajdah sahw if he adds extra rak'ahs.

رباعی نماز مسافر پر دو رکعت فرض ہیں، ان پر اضافہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ ہمارے نزدیک مسافر کے حق میں قصر رخصت اسقاط ہے، یعنی رباعی نماز میں دو رکعت ساقط ہو کر دو رکعت رہ گئی ہیں۔ امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ مسافر کے حق میں قصر رخصت ترفیہ ہے اور اتمام افضل ہے، یعنی مسافر کی سہولت کے پیش نظر اس کو دو رکعت پڑھنے کی اجازت دی گئی ہے، ورنہ رباعی نماز میں اس پر چار رکعت ہی فرض ہیں، اور چار ہی کا پڑھنا افضل ہے۔ اس کے قائل امام احمد ہیں اور امام مالک کا بھی ایک قول یہی ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل روزہ پر قیاس ہے، یعنی جس طرح مسافر کے لئے رمضان میں افطار کی اجازت ہے اور روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے، اسی طرح رباعی نماز میں قصر کی اجازت دی گئی ہے ورنہ اتمام افضل ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا: فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَنْ تَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَاةِ یعنی نماز کا قصر کرنے میں تم پر کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ آیت سے استدلال اس طور پر ہو گا کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے قصر کو لفظ لا جناح کے ساتھ مشروع کیا ہے، اور لفظ اباحت کے لئے ذکر کیا جاتا ہے نہ کہ وجوب کے لئے، جیسا کہ دوسری آیت میں ہے: لَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ إِنْ طَلَّقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ قصر مباح ہے، واجب نہیں۔ اور جب قصر کا مباح ہونا ثابت ہوا تو دوسرے مباحات کی طرح قصر کے اندر بھی مسافر کو اختیار ہو گا کہ قصر کرے یا اتمام کرے۔ تیسری دلیل حدیث عمر سے مروی ہے کہ یہ آیت مجھ پر مشتبہ ہوگئی تو میں نے رسول خدا سے دریافت کیا کہ کیا ہم قصر کریں حالانکہ ہم مامون ہیں، ہمیں کسی چیز کا خوف نہیں اور اللہ تعالیٰ نے ان خوف کو قصر کے ساتھ مشروط کیا ہے (یہ سن کر) آنحضرت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ یہ خداوند قدوس کی طرف سے صدقہ ہے، اللہ تعالیٰ کا صدقہ قبول کرو۔ حدیث میں قصر کو قبول کے ساتھ معلق کیا ہے اور قصر کا نام صدقہ رکھا ہے، اور قاعدہ ہے کہ جس پر صدقہ کیا جاتا ہے اس کو صدقہ میں اختیار
He said: The fard of the traveler in four-rak'ah prayers is two rak'ahs; he should not add to them. Al-Shafi'i said that his fard is four, and qasr is a concession, by analogy to fasting. Our evidence is that the second pair (shaf' thani) is neither made up nor is one sinful for leaving it, and this is a sign of it being nafl, unlike fasting because it is made up. Translation: Shaykh al-Quduri said that according to us, the traveler's four-rak'ah prayer is two rak'ahs; he should not add. Imam al-Shafi'i said that his fard is four, and qasr is a concession, by analogy to fasting. And our proof is that the second pair is not made up and one is not sinful for leaving it, indicating it is nafl, unlike fasting because it is made up. Explanation: Al-Quduri stated that according to us, the traveler's fard in four-rak'ah prayers is two rak'ahs, and adding is not allowed. The conclusion is that for us, qasr is a concession of reduction (isqat), meaning two rak'ahs are dropped and two remain. Al-Shafi'i said that qasr is a concession of permission (tarkhis) and completion is better, meaning for the traveler's ease he is allowed to pray two, but actually four are fard and praying four is better. Imam Ahmad holds this view, and Imam Malik also had one such view. Al-Shafi'i's proof is analogy to fasting: just as a traveler is allowed to break his fast and fasting is better, similarly qasr is allowed but completion is better. Second proof: Allah said 'There is no blame on you if you shorten the prayer.' The deduction is that the word 'junah' indicates permission, not obligation, as in the verse about divorce. So qasr is permissible, not obligatory, and the traveler has a choice. Third proof: Hadith of Umar who was confused about the verse and asked the Prophet whether they should shorten prayer while safe, and the Prophet said it is a charity from Allah, so accept His charity. The hadith attaches qasr to acceptance and calls it charity, and the principle is that one has choice in accepting charity.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the legal status of qasr (shortening prayer) for travelers. The author cites the matn of al-Quduri: 'The traveler's fard in four-rak'ah prayers is two rak'ahs; he should not add.' The Hanafi position is that qasr is a 'rukhsat isqat' (concession of reduction): the traveler's actual fard has been reduced from four to two; it is wajib to pray two, and adding to them invalidates the prayer (or at least makes it undesirable with tashahhud per other texts). Al-Shafi'i holds that qasr is a 'rukhsat tarkhis' (concession of permission): the fard remains four, but the traveler may shorten as a dispensation; completion is still better. The passage then presents the Hanafi proof: the second pair (the additional two rak'ahs) is not made up later if omitted, and omitting them incurs no sin, which indicates it is nafl (supererogatory). This is contrasted with fasting: if a traveler breaks his fast, it must be made up later, proving it remains obligatory on him. The passage then presents al-Shafi'i's three proofs: (1) analogy to fasting (qiyas); (2) the Quranic verse 'There is no blame on you' (la junah) which suggests permission, not obligation; (3) the hadith of Umar where the Prophet calls qasr a 'charity' (sadaqah) from Allah, indicating optional acceptance. The Hanafi response to these proofs is not fully recorded in the provided text (it is cut off). Typically, Hanafis argue that la junah in the Quran can indicate obligation in certain contexts (e.g., 'la junaha 'alaykum in talaqtum al-nisa' does not mean divorce is permissible anytime; context matters). Also, the hadith of charity is about accepting the ruling as a gift, not about having choice to pray four. However, the text ends mid-argument. The passage highlights that the practical difference is: Hanafis consider it wajib (obligatory) to shorten; Shafi'is consider it optional, with completion being better. The authority weight is from the sharh of al-Hidayah, reflecting the settled Hanafi position.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening four-rak'ah prayers to two rak'ahs during travel.
رخصة إسقاط (rukhsat isqat): A concession that lowers the original obligation; the fard itself is reduced.
رخصة ترفيه (rukhsat tarkhis): A concession that permits relaxation while the original obligation remains incumbent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is wajib, and the traveler should not pray four rak'ahs
C) It is recommended but not required
D) It is prohibited unless there is fear or hardship

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, qasr is wajib because the traveler's fard is two rak'ahs; the second pair is nafl, not a missed obligation.

Measuring Sea Travel Distance by Land Speed
Ruling on determining travel distance for sea journeys

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define masafat al-safar and apply the Hanafi rule that sea travel distance is measured by land travel speed to determine eligibility for travel concessions.

Core Idea
Sea travel distance is measured by land travel speed—three days and three nights on land define the traveler's concession.

This lesson clarifies a unique Hanafi principle: when traveling by sea, the distance that qualifies for travel concessions is not the actual sea time but the equivalent land distance. The commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah provides a concrete example to illustrate this rule. By the end, you will be able to apply this definition to real-world scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Term
Locate the term 'masafat al-safar' (مسافت سفر) in the passage and note that it refers to the distance that triggers travel concessions.
The passage defines it as the distance covered in three days and three nights on land.
2
Extract the Legal Definition
The Hanafi definition: travel distance is three days' journey on land (approx. 48 miles). This is derived from the base text (matn) of al-Hidayah.
The commentary emphasizes that this measure is fixed, regardless of actual travel time at sea.
3
Apply the Definition to Sea Travel
The passage gives an example: if a destination is two days by sea but three days by land, the land distance determines eligibility. The traveler must cover the land distance or its equivalent to qualify.
This shows that the measure is the land journey, not actual time.
4
State the Consequence
If the land distance is three days, the traveler qualifies for travel concessions (shortened prayer, etc.) even if the sea journey is shorter. If land distance is less, concessions are not granted.
This ruling ensures consistency across different terrains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلوة المسافر
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد دوم
موافق ہو نہ مخالف تو اس میں تین دن اور تین رات میں جس قدر مسافت طے کرے گا وہ مدت سفر کہلائے گی جس طرح پہاڑوں کے سفر میں تین دن اور تین رات کی مسافت معتبر ہے اگر چہ ہموار زمین میں اتنی مسافت اس سے کم مدت میں طے ہو جاتی ہو۔ متن کی عبارت کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ دریائی سفر میں خشکی کی رفتار معتبر نہ ہوگی مثلاً ایک مقام پر پہنچنے کے دو راستے ہیں ایک دریا کا دوسرا خشکی کا خشکی کے راستے میں اس مقام تک پہنچنے کے لئے تین دن اور تین رات کی مسافت ہے اور دریا کے راستہ سے دو یوم کی مسافت ہے پس اگر کوئی شخص یہ مسافت خشکی کے راستہ سے طے کرے گا تو اس کے لئے مسافروں کی رخصت حاصل ہوگی اور اگر دریائی راستہ سے گیا تو رخصت سفر حاصل نہ ہوگی۔
Chapter of the Traveler's Prayer. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu Sharh Hidayah Volume 2. Whether it is similar or not, in it the distance covered in three days and three nights is called the period of travel, just as in mountain travel the distance of three days and three nights is considered, even if on level ground that distance is covered in less time. The summary of the text's expression is that in sea travel, the speed of land is not considered. For example, to reach a place there are two routes: one by sea and the other by land. By the land route, the distance to that place is three days and three nights, and by the sea route it is two days' distance. So if a person covers this distance by the land route, he will obtain the concessions of a traveler, but if he goes by the sea route, he will not obtain the travel concession.

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling from the commentary on al-Hidayah. It clarifies how to determine travel distance for someone traveling by sea. The matn (base text) states that the travel distance is what can be covered in three days and three nights on land, regardless of actual time at sea. The commentary provides an example: if a destination is reachable in two days by sea but three days by land, the traveler must use the land distance to qualify for travel concessions. This ruling ensures consistency across different terrains and modes of travel. The Hanafi madhhab considers the travel distance (masafat al-safar) as approximately 48 miles (or three days' journey on foot), and this applies to sea travel by analogy to land travel. The key condition is that the distance must be measured by land travel speed, not by the actual time spent at sea.

Key Terms
مسافت سفر (masāfat al-safar): The distance that qualifies a person to be considered a traveler and gain concessions; defined as three days' journey on land.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Qasr Prayer: Reduction or License
Inter-madhhab dispute on qasr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qasr, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument and its evidence, and evaluate why the Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, qasr reduces the fard to two rak'ahs; for Shafi'is, it is a permissive license to shorten.

This lesson examines the classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of qasr (shortening prayer) during travel. The Hanafi school, as articulated by al-Quduri, holds that the traveler's fard in four-rak'ah prayers is only two rak'ahs—a true reduction (isqāt). Imam al-Shafi'i counters that the fard remains four, and qasr is merely a license (rukhṣah) to omit two, with completion being better. We will analyze the proofs on both sides and the practical implications of each view.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the Hanafi position (qasr is reduction, fard = 2 rak'ahs, adding prohibited) and the Shafi'i position (qasr is license, fard = 4 rak'ahs, shortening permissible).
Note the key terms: isqāt vs tarfiyya.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proofs
Analyze the three Shafi'i proofs: (1) qiyas to fasting, (2) the verse 'la junah' indicating permissibility, (3) the hadith calling qasr a charity.
Understand the logic behind each.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi school refutes each Shafi'i proof: qiyas fails because no qada' in qasr; the verse refers to shortening attributes, not rak'ahs; the charity hadith does not negate obligation.
The Hanafi counter-proof from 'Aisha, Ibn 'Abbas, and the practice of the caliphs.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: a traveler who prays four rak'ahs is sinful in Hanafi, but doing a better act in Shafi'i.
This shows how a difference in usul affects daily practice.

قصر نماز کی شرعی حیثیت
قال وفرض المسافر في الرباعية ركعتان لايزيد عليهما وقال الشافعى فرضه الأربع والقصر رخصة اعتبار بالصوم و لنا ان الشفع الثاني لا يقضى ولا يأثم على تركه و هذا آية النافلة بخلاف الصوم لانه يقضى . ترجمہ شیخ ( بخاری ومسلم ) حضرت عائشہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا کہ نماز دو دورکعت فرض کی گئی ہے پس سفر کی نماز کو اسی حال پر ) باقی رکھا گیا اور حضر کی نماز میں اضافہ کر دیا گیا۔ عن ابن س قال فرض الله الصلوة على لسان نبيكم فى الحضرا ربع ركعات ونى السفر رکعتین ابن عباس نے فرمایا کہ نالی نے تمہارے رسول کی زبانی مضر میں چار رکعتیں فرض کیں اور سفر میں دو رکعت طبرانی کی روایت ہے۔ افترض رسول الله سے اللہ علیہ وسلم رکعتين في السفر كما افترض في الحضر اربعا - رسول اللہ صلے اللہ علیہ وسلم نے سفر میں دور کھتیں کی ہیں۔ جیسا کہ حضر میں چار رکعت فرض کی ہیں لسانی و ابن ماجہ میں ہے عن ابن ابی لـيـلـي عـن عـمـر قال صلوة السفر الحان وصلوة الاضحى ركعتان وصلوة الفظر ركعتان وصلوة الجمعة ركعتان تمام غير قصر على السان عد حضرت عمر نے فرمایا کہ سفر کی نماز دورکعت میں عید الاضحی کی نماز دورکعت میں عید الفطر کی نماز دورکعت ہیں اور جمعہ کی نماز دورکعت اور یہ پوری نماز ہے بغیر قصر کئے پیغمبر خدا صلى الله عليه وسلم کی زبانی۔
بخاری شریف میں ابن عمر سے مروی ہے صحبت رسول الله في السفر لم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله سحبت عمر فلم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله و صحبت عثمان فلم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله وقد الله تعالى لَقَدْ كَانَ لَكُمْ فِي رَسُولِ اللهِ أسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ - ابن عمرؓ سے مروی ہے کہ میں سفر میں رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم کے ساتھ رہا انے دو رکعت پر زیادتی نہیں کی حتی کہ آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کا وصال ہو گیا اور والد محترم حضرت عمر کے ساتھ سفر میں رہا انہوں نے بھی دو ان پر اضافہ نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ آپ کا وصال ہو گیا۔ حضرت عثمان کے ساتھ سفر کیا آپ نے بھی تامین حیات دو رکعت پر اضافہ کیا۔ اور اللہ تعالے نے حضور مقبول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی حیات طیبہ کو اسوہ حسنہ فرمایا ہے۔ اس لئے اسی کا اتباع کیا جائے۔ ان تمام ایٹ سے سفر کی نماز کا دورکعت ہونا ثابت ہوتا ہے اگر سفر کی نماز میں چار رکعت پڑھنا افضل ہوتا جیسا کہ امام شافعی کا خیال ہے تو فرت اور آپ کے صحابہ اس فضیلت کو کبھی ترک نہ فرماتے۔ حضرت امام شافعی کے قیاس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مسافر کی قصر نماز کو اس کے روزہ پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے بلاشبہ مسافر کو ران میں افطار کی اجازت دی گئی ہے لیکن فرق ہے وہ یہ کہ مسافر پر رباعی کے اندر قصر کرنے کی صورت میں اخرین کی قضاء واجب نہیں اور روزہ کی قضا واجب ہے پس اس فرق کے ساتھ ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا کیسے درست ہوگا۔ حاصل یہ کہ کسی چیز کو اس حال میں ا کہ نہ اس کا بدل واجب ہو نہ اس کے ترک پر گناہ ہو تو یہ اس چیز کے نفل ہونے کی علامت ہے رہا روزہ تو اس کا ترک بلا بدل نہیں ہے ل کا بدل موجود ہے یعنی قضاء۔ امام شافعی کی طرف سے پیش کردہ آیت کا جواب یہ ہے کہ آیت میں اوصاف کا قصر مراد ہے یعنی خوف کی وجہ سے قیام کو چھوڑ کر قعود اختیار نارکوع و سجود کو چھوڑ کر اشارہ کے ساتھ نماز پڑھنا اور ہمارے نزدیک خوف کے وقت اوصاف کا قصر ہے واجب نہیں ہے۔ پس جب آیت میں اوصات کا قصر مراد ہے تو اس سے رکعات کے قصر پر استدلال نہیں کیا جاسکتا۔
The legal status of qasr. He said: The fard for the traveler in the four-rak'ah prayer is two rak'ahs, he should not add to them. Al-Shafi'i said: His fard is four, and shortening is a license, by analogy to fasting. Our proof is that the second pair is neither made up (qada') nor is one sinful for abandoning it, and this is a sign of it being supererogatory (nafl), unlike fasting because it is made up. Translation: Shaykh al-Quduri said: For the traveler, the four-rak'ah prayer is two rak'ahs; he should not add. Imam al-Shafi'i said: His fard is four rak'ahs, and shortening is a license by analogy to fasting. And our proof is that the second pair is neither made up nor is one sinful for leaving it, and this is a sign of it being nafl, unlike fasting because its qada' is required. Explanation: Al-Quduri said: In our view, the four-rak'ah prayer on the traveler is two rak'ahs fard; adding is not permissible. In short, in our view, the license of qasr for the traveler is reduction (isqat); i.e., two rak'ahs are dropped, leaving two. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The license of qasr for the traveler is concession (tarfiya), and completion is better; i.e., due to the traveler's ease, he is permitted to pray two rak'ahs, otherwise four are fard upon him, and praying four is better. Imam Ahmad is of this view, and Imam Malik also had one such saying. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is analogy to fasting: just as the traveler is permitted to break fast in Ramadan and not fasting is better, similarly in the four-rak'ah prayer, shortening is permitted, otherwise completion is better. Second proof: Allah says: 'There is no sin upon you if you shorten the prayer' (4:101). The deduction from the verse is that Allah legislated qasr with the word 'la junah', which indicates permissibility (ibaha), not obligation, as in another verse: 'There is no sin upon you if you divorce women' (2:236). Thus qasr is permissible, not obligatory. When qasr is established as permissible, the traveler has choice to shorten or complete. Third proof: Hadith from 'Umar: He was confused about the verse and asked the Prophet: 'We shorten while we are secure?' The Prophet said: 'It is a charity from Allah, so accept His charity.' The hadith attaches qasr to acceptance and calls it charity, and the rule is that one is not obligated to accept charity. (Fath al-Qadir). Our proof: If the traveler shortens and leaves the last two rak'ahs, they are not made up after residence, nor is he sinful for abandoning them. The absence of required qada' and sin is a sign of nafl. Thus, only two rak'ahs are obligatory upon the traveler. Second textual proof: From 'Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs, then the travel prayer was left as it was, and the resident prayer was increased.' (Bukhari and Muslim). From Ibn 'Abbas: 'Allah prescribed prayer on the tongue of your Prophet: in residence four rak'ahs, and in travel two.' Tabarani narrated: 'The Messenger of Allah prescribed two rak'ahs in travel just as He prescribed four in residence.' Al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah have from Ibn Abi Layla from 'Umar: 'The travel prayer is two, the 'Eid al-Adha prayer is two, the 'Eid al-Fitr prayer is two, the Friday prayer is two, complete without shortening upon the tongue of the Prophet.' In Sahih al-Bukhari from Ibn 'Umar: 'I accompanied the Prophet in travel and he never exceeded two rak'ahs until Allah took him. I accompanied Abu Bakr and he never exceeded two until Allah took him. I accompanied 'Umar and he never exceeded two until Allah took him. I accompanied 'Uthman and he never exceeded two until Allah took him.' And Allah says: 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' (33:21). All these prove that the travel prayer is two rak'ahs. If praying four in travel were better as Imam al-Shafi'i thinks, the Prophet and his Companions would never have abandoned that virtue. The answer to Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy is that comparing qasr prayer to fasting is invalid because the traveler is permitted to break fast but must make it up, whereas in prayer qasr does not require making up the omitted rak'ahs. The difference is that in qasr there is no replacement (badal) and no sin, which indicates it is nafl. As for fasting, its abandonment without replacement is not allowed; replacement (qada') is required. The response to the verse presented by Shafi'i is that the verse refers to shortening of attributes (awṣāf), i.e., in fear, leaving standing and sitting and pointing, not shortening of rak'ahs. In our view, shortening attributes in fear is permissible (not obligatory). So the verse cannot be used as proof for shortening the number of rak'ahs.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on the nature of qasr (shortening) prayer for travelers. The commentary expands the matn of al-Quduri. The Hanafi position, stated by al-Quduri, is that for the traveler, the fard in four-rak'ah prayers is only two rak'ahs; he must not add. This is a reduction (isqat), meaning two rak'ahs are dropped entirely. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the fard remains four, but qasr is a license (rukhsah) to shorten; completing four is better. The Shafi'i proof is qiyas (analogy) to the traveler's fasting: just as he may break fast but it is better to fast, similarly he may shorten but completing is better. He also cites the verse 'la junah' to imply permissibility, and the hadith from 'Umar calling qasr a charity (sadaqah) which one is not obliged to accept. The Hanafi response first refutes the qiyas: in fasting, the traveler must make up missed days (qada'), but in qasr, the omitted two rak'ahs require no qada' and incur no sin—this shows they are not obligatory. The Hanafi proof is textual: hadith from 'Aisha that prayer was originally prescribed as two, then travel prayer remained two while resident prayer increased. Also, Ibn 'Abbas states Allah made fard four in residence and two in travel. The practice of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthman in always praying two in travel is cited. The verse is interpreted as referring to shortening of actions (like standing, bowing) due to fear, not number of rak'ahs. The tarjih (preferred view) is the Hanafi position, as it is based on explicit texts and consistent practice of the early caliphs.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the prayer; for Hanafi it means reducing the fard from four to two rak'ahs; for Shafi'i it is a license to pray two instead of four.
رباعية (rubā‘iyya): A prayer with four rak'ahs in residence, such as Zuhr, 'Asr, and 'Isha'.
شفع ثاني (shaf‘ thānī): The second pair of rak'ahs (i.e., the last two rak'ahs) in a four-rak'ah prayer.
اسقاط (isqāṭ): Dropping; the Hanafi concept that the fard is reduced by dropping two rak'ahs.
رخصت (rukhṣat): A concession or license granted in exceptional circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the status of the last two rak'ahs when a traveler prays Zuhr?
A) They are fard and must be prayed.
B) They are sunnah (mustahab).
C) They are nafl and not required.
D) They are wajib but can be omitted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that qasr is a reduction (isqāt) making two rak'ahs fard for the traveler is mufta bihi, supported by explicit hadith and the consistent practice of the Prophet and the Rightly-Guided Caliphs.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
15 min
The Obligation of Qasr: Hanafi Proofs and Refutation of Shafi'i
Presenting Hanafi proofs for obligatory qasr and refuting Shafi'i arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that qasr is wajib and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Qasr is not a concession—it is the wajib prayer for the traveler, proven by the Prophet's practice and the nature of obligation.

The obligation of shortening prayer (qasr) during travel is a point of disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While the Hanafis consider qasr to be wajib (obligatory) and completing four rak'ahs sinful without excuse, the Shafi'is view it as merely permissible (mubah) with completion being better. This lesson examines the Hanafi proofs from hadith, reason, and legal theory, and addresses the Shafi'i arguments from the Qur'an, qiyas, and the hadith of sadaqah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school rules qasr as wajib for travelers on four-rak'ah prayers. Note that it is not fard because the evidence is from ahad hadith.
Wajib here means omission is sinful but the prayer is still valid (requires compensation if omitted).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qasr is mubah (permissible) and that performing tamam (four rak'ahs) is better. We must understand this contrast clearly.
The practical difference is whether the traveler is obligated to shorten or may choose.
3
Follow the Hanafi proofs
Examine the rational proof (no qada, no sin → second pair not obligatory) and the multiple hadith from Aisha, Ibn Abbas, Umar, and Ibn Umar.
These hadith show the Prophet and caliphs consistently performed two rak'ahs in travel.
4
Analyse the Hanafi refutations of Shafi'i arguments
Three Shafi'i arguments are refuted: (1) qiyas to fasting (rejected due to difference in qada), (2) the Quranic verse (la junah is used for obligation; verse refers to attributes, not number), (3) the hadith of sadaqah (sadaqah here is isqat, not tamlik, so it is obligatory).
Each refutation is based on usul al-fiqh principles.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi, the traveler must shorten; under Shafi'i, he may choose. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Hanafi position.
This difference affects daily practice of millions of Muslims.

ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مسافر اگر قصر کرے اور آخری دو رکعتوں کو ترک کر دے تو نہ ان کی قضاء کی جاتی ہے اور نہ ہی ان کے چھوڑنے پر گنہگار ہوتا ہے اور قضاء کا واجب نہ ہونا اور گنہگار نہ ہونا شفع ثانی کے ہونے کی علامت ہے پس ثابت ہوا کہ مسافر پر رباعی نماز میں فقط دو رکعتیں واجب ہیں۔ دوسری نقلی دلیل عن عائشة قالت فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين فقرت صلاة السفر وزيدت في الحضر. (بخاری ومسلم) حضرت عائشہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا کہ نماز دو دو رکعت فرض کی گئی پس سفر کی نماز کو اسی حال پر باقی رکھا گیا اور حضر کی نماز میں اضافہ کر دیا گیا۔ عن ابن عباس قال فرض الله الصلاة على لسان نبيكم في الحضر أربع ركعات وفي السفر ركعتين۔ ابن عباس نے فرمایا کہ اللہ نے تمہارے رسول کی زبانی حضر میں چار رکعتیں فرض کیں اور سفر میں دو رکعت۔ طبرانی کی روایت ہے: افترض رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ركعتين في السفر كما افترض في الحضر أربعاً۔ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے سفر میں دو رکعتیں فرض کی ہیں جیسا کہ حضر میں چار رکعت فرض کی ہیں۔ نسائی و ابن ماجہ میں ہے عن ابن أبي ليلى عن عمر قال صلاة السفر ركعتان وصلاة الأضحى ركعتان وصلاة الفطر ركعتان وصلاة الجمعة ركعتان تمام غير قصر على لسان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم۔ حضرت عمر نے فرمایا کہ سفر کی نماز دو رکعت، عید الاضحی کی نماز دو رکعت، عید الفطر کی نماز دو رکعت اور جمعہ کی نماز دو رکعت، اور یہ پوری نماز ہے بغیر قصر کے پیغمبر خدا صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی زبانی۔ بخاری شریف میں ابن عمر سے مروی ہے: صحبت رسول الله في السفر لم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله، وصحبت عمر فلم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله، وصحبت عثمان فلم يزد على ركعتين حتى قبضه الله، وقد قال الله تعالى: لَقَدْ كَانَ لَكُمْ فِي رَسُولِ اللهِ أُسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ۔ ابن عمرؓ سے مروی ہے کہ میں سفر میں رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے ساتھ رہا، آپ نے دو رکعت پر زیادتی نہیں کی حتی کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا وصال ہو گیا، اور والد محترم حضرت عمر کے ساتھ سفر میں رہا، انہوں نے بھی دو پر اضافہ نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ آپ کا وصال ہو گیا، حضرت عثمان کے ساتھ سفر کیا، آپ نے بھی تمام حیات دو رکعت پر اضافہ نہیں کیا۔ اور اللہ تعالیٰ نے حضور مقبول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی حیات طیبہ کو اسوہ حسنہ فرمایا ہے۔ اس لئے اسی کا اتباع کیا جائے۔ ان تمام احادیث سے سفر کی نماز کا دو رکعت ہونا ثابت ہوتا ہے، اگر سفر کی نماز میں چار رکعت پڑھنا افضل ہوتا جیسا کہ امام شافعی کا خیال ہے تو نبی اور آپ کے صحابہ اس فضیلت کو کبھی ترک نہ فرماتے۔ حضرت امام شافعی کے قیاس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مسافر کی قصر نماز کو اس کے روزہ پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے بلاشبہ مسافر کو روزہ میں افطار کی اجازت دی گئی ہے لیکن فرق ہے وہ یہ کہ مسافر پر رباعی کے اندر قصر کرنے کی صورت میں اخیرین کی قضاء واجب نہیں اور روزہ کی قضا واجب ہے، پس اس فرق کے ساتھ ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا کیسے درست ہوگا۔ حاصل یہ کہ کسی چیز کو اس حال میں کہ نہ اس کا بدل واجب ہو نہ اس کے ترک پر گناہ ہو تو یہ اس چیز کے نفل ہونے کی علامت ہے، رہا روزہ تو اس کا ترک بلا بدل نہیں ہے بلکہ بدل موجود ہے یعنی قضاء۔ امام شافعی کی طرف سے پیش کردہ آیت کا جواب یہ ہے کہ آیت میں اوصاف کا قصر مراد ہے یعنی خوف کی وجہ سے قیام کو چھوڑ کر قعود اختیار کرنا، رکوع و سجود کو چھوڑ کر اشارہ کے ساتھ نماز پڑھنا، اور ہمارے نزدیک خوف کے وقت اوصاف کا قصر واجب نہیں ہے۔ پس جب آیت میں اوصاف کا قصر مراد ہے تو اس سے رکعات کے قصر پر استدلال نہیں کیا جاسکتا۔ اور اگر تسلیم کر لیا جائے کہ آیت میں اصل نماز کا قصر مراد ہے تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ امام شافعی کا یہ کہنا کہ لفظ "لا جناح" اباحت کے لئے ذکر کیا جاتا ہے، وجوب کے لئے نہیں، غلط ہے کیونکہ آیت إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِنْ شَعَائِرِ اللهِ فَمَنْ حَجَّ الْبَيْتَ أَوِ اعْتَمَرَ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَطَّوَّفَ بِهِمَا میں "لا جناح" سے سعی بین الصفا والمروہ کے وجوب کو ثابت کیا گیا ہے، خود امام شافعی بھی اس موقع پر اباحت مراد نہیں لیتے۔ تشریح: قصر پڑھنا واجب ہے جیسا کہ جلالین میں مذکور ہے۔ امام شافعی کی پیش کردہ حدیث عمر کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہ حدیث ہماری دلیل ہے نہ کہ آپ کی، اس لئے کہ حدیث کے اندر "فاقبلوا" کا صیغہ امر ہے اور امر وجوب کے لئے آتا ہے، پس قصر کو جس کو صدقہ کہا گیا ہے اس کا قبول کرنا واجب ہوا نہ کہ مباح۔ دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ صدقہ دو طرح کا ہوتا ہے: ایک تملیکات کے قبیلہ سے جیسے مال کا صدقہ، دوم اسقاطات کے قبیلہ سے جیسے عتاق (آزاد کرنا) اور قصاص کو معاف کرنا۔ قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جو صدقہ تملیکات کے قبیلہ سے ہو اگر اس کو رد کر دیا جائے تو وہ رد ہو جائے گا، البتہ جو اسقاطات کے قبیلہ سے ہو رد کرنے سے رد نہیں ہوتا۔ پس قصر صلاة ایسا صدقہ ہے جواز قبیل اسقاطات ہے، لہذا یہ رد کرنے سے رد نہیں ہوگا اور جب متصدق علیہ کے رد کرنے سے رد نہیں ہوا تو گویا واجب ہوا۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ قصر واجب ہے۔
Our proof is that if a traveler shortens prayer and abandons the last two rak'ahs, qada is not required for them nor is he sinful for leaving them. The fact that qada is not obligatory and he is not sinful is a sign that the second pair (al-shaf' al-thani) does not exist. So it is established that only two rak'ahs are obligatory upon the traveler in the four-rak'ah prayer. Another transmitted proof: from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs, two rak'ahs. Then the prayer of travel was left as it was, and an increase was made for the prayer of residence.' (Bukhari and Muslim). From Ibn Abbas: 'Allah prescribed prayer upon the tongue of your Prophet: in residence four rak'ahs, and in travel two rak'ahs.' Narrated by Tabarani: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prescribed two rak'ahs in travel just as he prescribed four in residence.' In Nasa'i and Ibn Majah from Ibn Abi Layla from Umar: 'The prayer of travel is two rak'ahs, the prayer of Eid al-Adha is two rak'ahs, the prayer of Eid al-Fitr is two rak'ahs, and the prayer of Jumu'ah is two rak'ahs, complete, not shortened, upon the tongue of the Prophet (peace be upon him).' In Bukhari from Ibn Umar: 'I accompanied the Messenger of Allah in travel and he never exceeded two rak'ahs until Allah took him. I accompanied Abu Bakr and he never exceeded two rak'ahs until Allah took him. I accompanied Umar and he never exceeded two rak'ahs until Allah took him. And Allah said: "Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example."' All these hadith prove that travel prayer is two rak'ahs. If four rak'ahs were better in travel as Imam Shafi'i thinks, the Prophet and his Companions would never have abandoned that virtue. The response to Imam Shafi'i's qiyas is that it is incorrect to analogize the traveler's shortening of prayer to his fasting. For indeed the traveler is permitted to break fast in Ramadan, but there is a difference: in the case of shortening prayer, the traveler does not have to make up the last two rak'ahs, whereas in fasting, qada is obligatory. So how can one be analogized to the other with this difference? The outcome is that a thing which neither requires a substitute nor incurs sin for its abandonment is a sign that it is supererogatory. As for fasting, its abandonment is not without a substitute; rather a substitute exists, i.e., qada. Regarding the verse presented by Imam Shafi'i, the response is that the verse intends shortening of attributes (khawf), i.e., abandoning standing for sitting, and abandoning bowing and prostration for gestures. And in our view, shortening of attributes during fear is not obligatory. So when the verse intends shortening of attributes, it cannot be used to argue for shortening the number of rak'ahs. And if it is conceded that the verse intends shortening of the actual prayer, then we say that Imam Shafi'i's claim that the term 'la junah' is used for permissibility, not obligation, is incorrect, because in the verse 'Indeed al-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah. So whoever performs Hajj or 'Umrah, there is no blame upon him to walk between them' the term 'la junah' is used to establish the obligation of sa'i between al-Safa and al-Marwah. Imam Shafi'i himself does not take permissibility in that context. Clarification: Qasr is obligatory as mentioned in Jalalayn. The response to Imam Shafi'i's hadith of Umar is that this hadith is our proof, not yours, because the phrase 'faqbilu' in the hadith is an imperative form, and the imperative indicates obligation. So accepting the charity (sadaqah) called qasr is obligatory, not permissible. A second response is that charity is of two types: one from the category of making property ownership (tamlikat), such as charity of wealth, and the other from the category of waiving rights (isqatat), such as manumission and forgiving retaliation. The rule is that charity from the category of tamlikat, if rejected, it is rejected. But charity from isqatat is not rejected by rejection. So qasr prayer is a charity from the category of isqatat. Therefore it is not rejected by the recipient's rejection, and since it is not rejected, it becomes obligatory. So it is established that qasr is obligatory.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam Shafi'i on the legal status of qasr (shortening prayer) for travelers. The author begins by presenting the Hanafi proof that qasr is obligatory (wajib), not merely permissible. The core Hanafi argument is rational and transmitted: if a traveler prays only two rak'ahs (qasr) and omits the last two, he is not required to make them up and incurs no sin — this indicates that the second pair was never wajib for him. Therefore only two rak'ahs are obligatory in a four-rak'ah prayer for the traveler. Then the author cites multiple hadith: Aisha's report that prayer was originally prescribed as two rak'ahs, and travel prayer was left unchanged; Ibn Abbas's statement that Allah prescribed two in travel and four in residence; Umar's statement that travel prayer is two rak'ahs, complete, not shortened; and Ibn Umar's report that the Prophet and the first three caliphs never exceeded two rak'ahs in travel. These hadith establish that qasr is the prescribed norm. The author then refutes Imam Shafi'i's view that qasr is preferable to perform tamam (completing four) by noting that if it were better, the Prophet and Companions would not have consistently performed qasr. He then addresses three specific Shafi'i arguments: (1) Qiyas of prayer to fasting: rejected because fasting requires qada, qasr does not. (2) The Quranic verse 'There is no blame upon you for shortening prayer' (Al-Nisa' 4:101): the author argues that 'la junah' is used for obligation elsewhere (safa and marwa), and that the verse refers to shortening attributes due to fear, not number of rak'ahs. (3) The hadith calling qasr a 'sadaqah' (charity) with the command 'faqbilu' (accept): the author says that the imperative indicates obligation, and even if it were charity, it is from the category of isqatat (waiver) which cannot be rejected, so it becomes obligatory. Thus the Hanafi position that qasr is wajib is firmly established.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayers to two rak'ahs when traveling.
صدقہ (sadaqah): Here, a metaphorical charity from Allah granting the traveler the concession of shortening prayer.
اسقاطات (isqatat): Waivers or relinquishments of rights, e.g., forgiving retaliation or manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal status of qasr (shortening prayer) for a traveler?
A) It is sunnah mu'akkadah.
B) It is wajib (obligatory).
C) It is mubah (permissible).
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
Qasr is wajib for the traveler in the Hanafi school; the Shafi'i view that it is merely mubah is refuted by hadith and the nature of the obligation.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Traveler Praying Four Rak'ahs: Sitting in the Second Rak'ah
Ruling on traveler praying four rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two possible rulings when a traveler prays four rak'ahs instead of two, and explain why sitting for tashahhud in the second rak'ah is the decisive condition.

Core Idea
A traveler's four-rak'ah prayer is valid but sinful if he sits; invalid if he doesn't.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi school: a traveler who intentionally prays four rak'ahs instead of the shortened two. The ruling hinges on whether he pauses in the second rak'ah for tashahhud. This case illustrates the critical Hanafi principle that mixing an obligatory act with a supererogatory one before completing the obligatory pillars invalidates the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two possible outcomes
The traveler prays four rak'ahs instead of two. The text distinguishes two cases: (1) he sits in the second rak'ah for tashahhud, (2) he does not sit. Note the different rulings.
Sitting here means pausing long enough to recite the tashahhud.
2
Locate the proof and its structure
The text uses analogy (qiyas) to the Fajr prayer. Understand that if someone prays four rak'ahs for Fajr and sits in the second, the first two are Fajr fard and the last two are nafl.
The analogy shows that the first two rak'ahs are the fard; the last two are nafl.
3
Analyze the condition for validity
The sitting in the second rak'ah serves as the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) for the fard prayer, completing its pillars. Without it, the fard's pillars are incomplete, and mixing nafl with the incomplete fard invalidates the prayer.
The final sitting is a rukn (pillar) in the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the consequence of delaying salam
Even when valid, the traveler sins for delaying the salam after completing the fard. The fard ends with the sitting; he should have said salam immediately. The sin does not affect validity.
This is a wajib (obligatory) element that was delayed.
5
Recognize the Hanafi reliance on this ruling
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is the mufta bihi (operative position). There is no intra-Hanafi disagreement. The Shafi'i school differs because they permit itmam in travel, so this scenario would not arise in their school.
Hanafis hold that qasr is wajib for the traveler; Shafi'is say it is permissible but not required.

Source Text
